Boris Burkov

2 items
Patches Submitted (1)
Last comment 2026-06-16 [PATCH v3] btrfs: release extent lock per folio in readahead Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch modifies the Btrfs file system's readahead behavior to release extent locks per folio, rather than holding them for the entire range. This change is intended to prevent a race condition between readahead and direct IO writes, which can cause tasks to hang on the extent lock. The problem arises when readahead fails due to memory allocation issues, leaving pages in an inconsistent state and causing subsequent reads to block on the extent lock. By releasing locks per folio, the patch aims to alleviate this issue and reduce stress on the system.

Reviews Given (1)
Last comment 2026-06-15 Re: [PATCH v2] btrfs: release extent lock per folio in readahead Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch addresses a contention issue on the extent lock in Btrfs, which occurs when multiple tasks are reading an overlapping range of a compressed file and one task is stuck in direct reclaim. The patch releases the extent lock per folio in readahead to alleviate some stress and prevent one thread's slowdown from escalating to others. This change is not critical but can help improve system health on large, loaded systems with high memory pressure.

Dmitry Ilvokhin

5 items
Patches Submitted (4)
[PATCH 0/3] perf record: fix multi-record Zstd compression Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch series fixes a regression in perf record's Zstandard compression feature, which caused it to abort recording and produce an undecompressable file due to a 'Bad address' error. The patches also fix two latent bugs: an output buffer overrun and a broken error fallback in the zstd compressor. The changes ensure that multiple compressed records are handled correctly and that raw data is not stored when compression fails.

[PATCH 1/3] perf record: Fix multiple PERF_RECORD_COMPRESSED2 records per push Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch fixes a bug in perf record where multiple PERF_RECORD_COMPRESSED2 records are emitted per push when Zstd compression is enabled and the compressed output exceeds the maximum record size. The fix involves changing how the header size is calculated to prevent underflow and truncation, allowing for correct decompression of the data. The patch also updates the test case to reflect this change.

[PATCH 2/3] perf record: Avoid overrunning the zstd output buffer Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch fixes a bug in the zstd compression API used by perf tools, where the output buffer could be overrun when compressing data. The issue occurred because the function did not check if there was enough space left in the output buffer to hold a full record before writing one. The patch adds a check to prevent this overwriting and returns an error instead.

[PATCH 3/3] perf record: Don't store raw data on zstd compression failure Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch fixes a bug in the perf record tool's ZSTD compression handling. When ZSTD compression fails, it previously attempted to copy the uncompressed data into the output buffer, which can lead to issues such as incorrect header sizes and unbounded copying. Instead, this patch propagates the error by returning -1 when compression fails, preventing these problems.

  • [8/9] dax/kmem: add sysfs interface for atomic hotplug Neutral Submitted 2026-06-05Last comment 2026-06-09 David (Arm) Gregory Price (author)
  • [9/9] selftests/dax: add dax/kmem hotplug sysfs regression test Submitted 2026-06-05
Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series adds atomic whole-device hotplug control via sysfs, allowing for transitions to be atomic across entire device ranges. The patch addresses a race condition between offlining and removing memory blocks, which was discussed in the LPC2025 device memory sessions. It introduces a 'hotplug' attribute and updates mm and dax plumbing to support it. The state names mirror the per-block memoryX/state ABI with modifications for atomic whole-device control. The patch also includes mm preparation changes, such as adding helpers for online type and offline+remove of multiple ranges under a single lock.

Last comment 2026-06-15 Re: [PATCH v2] cgroup/cpuset: rebind mm mempolicy to effective_mems, not mems_allowed Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch updates how memory policies are bound to CPU sets in the Linux kernel. It changes the binding from `mems_allowed` to `effective_mems`, which is necessary because `mems_allowed` is protected by a task lock and cannot be accessed for foreign tasks. This fix resolves inconsistencies and potential SIGBUS errors that can occur when interacting with memory policies and CPU sets.

5 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants

Waiman Long ↳ replying to Gregory Price Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Waiman Long noted that the patch's use of effective_mems instead of mems_allowed could lead to inconsistent behavior during the transition period when a memory node is being hotunplugged, and suggested reverting to using cs->effective_mems to maintain the old cgroup v1 behavior.
Show original comment 
That is what I believe why mpol_rebind_mm() a bit differently from the 
others and it is historically done this way a long time ago before 
cgroup v2.

For cgroup v1, mems_allowed can't be empty or you can't put any task 
into the cpuset. Also effective_mems is the same as mems_allowed. cgroup 
v2 is quite different in how it handles memory nodes and CPUs. Users can 
isn't forced to set mems_allowed and cpus_allowed as effective_mems and 
effective_cpus will inherit parent version if mems_allowed and 
cpus_allowed are not set. IOW, effective_mems will never be empty. Yes, 
it is a bug with the introduction of cpuset v2 that we should have 
replaced mems_allowed by effective_mems at that time. With v2, 
effective_mems should contain only online nodes. The only exception is 
during the short transition period when a memory node hotunplug 
operation is in progress when a write to cpuset.mems is happening at the 
same time. With v1, it is theoretically possible that none of the nodes 
in mems_allowed is online.

The reason why I am suggesting to use cs->effective_mems to keep the old 
cgroup v1 behavior. If the consensus is to use the output of 
guarantee_online_mems() for mpol_rebind_mm(), I will not be against that 
but it will be a slight change in user-visible behavior.

Cheers, Longman

BTW, I still prefer the v2 patch. If it is decided we should use the

patch with changes in the relevant documentation like 
Documentation/admin-guide/cgroup-v1/cpuset.rst.

virtio-balloon patch for stats push mode, discussion around latency and thread efficiency

Gregory Price Neutral LLM
Raised concerns about latency and thread efficiency in virtio-balloon stats push mode, suggesting a timer-based approach like QEMU's. Identified potential issues with blocked threads and reaping guests.
Show original comment 
On Tue, Jun 16, 2026 at 04:32:46PM +0200, David Hildenbrand (Arm) wrote:
> On 6/16/26 15:57, Gregory Price wrote:
> > 
> > Definitely an RFC here because I'm not sure if I was missing something
> > that might help me solve the problem.
> 
> Well, in QEMU we just run a timer internally that does the polling.
> 
> Then, upper layers in the stack can ask QEMU for the latest stats.
> 
> There, you just get the stats along with a "last-update" timestamp.
>

That makes sense, although don't you just push the blocking operation
into yet another thread on the host?

Assuming it's not cancel-able, there's a blocked thread there you have
to reap.  Vs the guest being unresponsive and not sending updates, you
just reap the whole guest or take some other corrective action.

I suppose to the orchestrator reaping QEMU and reaping the guest looks
the same.  The difference is just where the thread lives, hmmmm

I'll make a note to inspect QEMU's solution to see if that's handled
or if it would be subject to the same issue.

Thanks!
~Gregory

negotiate extensions to the original directory delegations originally

In practice, the Linux nfs server requires that the client support this

Chuck Lever ↳ replying to Jeff Layton Needs Work LLM
Identified a build breakage in an NFSv3-only server with CONFIG_PROC_FS=n due to missing stubs for svc_stat_alloc_counts() and svc_stat_free_counts(). Suggested moving the functions to net/sunrpc/svc.c.
Show original comment 
Hey Jeff -

On Tue, Jun 16, 2026, at 8:45 AM, Jeff Layton wrote:
> The existing per-procedure call counts live in global
> svc_version->vs_count[] arrays which are not network-namespace-aware.
> Add per-netns equivalents in struct svc_stat so the upcoming netlink
> stats interface can return namespace-scoped statistics.
>
> Add a vs_count pointer array to struct svc_stat, along with
> svc_stat_alloc_counts() and svc_stat_free_counts() helpers to manage
> per-version percpu call count arrays.

This patch appears to break the build for an NFSv3-only server
configured with CONFIG_PROC_FS=n.

svc_stat_alloc_counts() and svc_stat_free_counts() are defined in
net/sunrpc/stats.c, which the Makefile builds only under PROC_FS:

       sunrpc-$(CONFIG_PROC_FS) += stats.o

nfsd_net_init()/nfsd_net_exit() call both helpers unconditionally,
and the declarations in <linux/sunrpc/stats.h> sit outside the
#ifdef CONFIG_PROC_FS block with no !PROC_FS stubs. CONFIG_NFSD does
not depend on PROC_FS -- only NFSD_V4 does -- so NFSD=y with
NFSD_V2/V3 and PROC_FS=n is a valid Kconfig combination. The result
is an undefined reference to svc_stat_alloc_counts at vmlinux link
(NFSD=y), or a modpost failure for NFSD=m since both symbols are
EXPORT_SYMBOL_GPL.

net/sunrpc/svc.c is always built, and might be a more natural home
for the new functions.

-- 
Chuck Lever
Signals: build breakage
Chuck Lever ↳ replying to Jeff Layton Needs Work LLM
Noted that the netlink dump handler drops a counter (wdeleg_getattr) and suggested adding it to the server-stats attribute-set in nfsd.yaml.
Show original comment 
Hey Jeff -

On Tue, Jun 16, 2026, at 8:45 AM, Jeff Layton wrote:
> Implement nfsd_nl_server_stats_get_dumpit() which exposes the
> NFS server statistics currently available via /proc/net/rpc/nfsd
> through the nfsd generic netlink family.
>
> The handler uses a dump operation to stream statistics across
> multiple netlink messages:
>
>   - First message: all scalar stats (reply cache, filehandle,
>     IO, network, RPC) plus per-version procedure counts
>     (proc2/3/4-ops) using per-netns vs_count arrays.
>
>   - Subsequent messages: NFSv4 per-operation counts
>     (proc4ops-ops), one entry per message, using cb->args[0]
>     to track the current operation index across dump calls.
>
> This allows nfsstat to retrieve server statistics via netlink
> with a procfs fallback for older kernels.
>
> Assisted-by: Claude:claude-opus-4-6
> Signed-off-by: Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org>
> ---
>  Documentation/netlink/specs/nfsd.yaml | 105 +++++++++++++++++
>  fs/nfsd/netlink.c                     |   5 +
>  fs/nfsd/netlink.h                     |   2 +
>  fs/nfsd/nfsctl.c                      | 206 ++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++
>  include/uapi/linux/nfsd_netlink.h     |  35 ++++++
>  5 files changed, 353 insertions(+)

The procfs output in nfsd_show() emits one more counter that this dump
handler drops: wdeleg_getattr (NFSD_STATS_WDELEG_GETATTR), printed at
the end of the CONFIG_NFSD_V4 block in fs/nfsd/stats.c, right after
proc4ops. The netlink dump stops after proc4ops-ops and sets
cb->args[0] = -1, so the write-delegation GETATTR-conflict counter never
goes out on the wire.

There's no schema slot for it either: the server-stats attribute-set
ends at proc4ops-ops, so a consumer that prefers netlink and only falls
back to procfs on an old kernel loses this counter on every kernel new
enough to support server-stats-get.

I Suggest:

- add a wdeleg-getattr (u64) attribute to the server-stats
  attribute-set in nfsd.yaml and list it under the dump reply
  attributes;

- add NFSD_A_SERVER_STATS_WDELEG_GETATTR to the uapi enum, after
  PROC4OPS_OPS and before __NFSD_A_SERVER_STATS_MAX so existing
  values don't shift;

- emit it as a single scalar in the start == 0 message, wrapped in
  CONFIG_NFSD_V4 to match the procfs side.


-- 
Chuck Lever
Signals: missing counter

Chuck Lever Needs Work LLM
Raised a potential use-after-free (UAF) possibility in the patch and suggested addressing it before application.
Show original comment 

On Thu, Jun 18, 2026, at 12:57 PM, Jeff Layton wrote:
> The NFS server tracks per-operation call counts for the forward channel
> (proc4ops) but keeps no statistics for the NFSv4 backchannel (callback)
> operations it sends to clients.
>
> Add a per-netns array of percpu counters for callback operations, indexed
> by RFC 8881 callback opcode (OP_CB_GETATTR..OP_CB_OFFLOAD), and bump the
> relevant counter in nfsd4_run_cb(), which is hit exactly once per callback
> that is actually queued.
>
> CB_GETATTR is sent when a GETATTR conflicts with an outstanding write
> delegation, which is roughly what the dedicated wdeleg_getattr counter
> tracked. The two are not identical: the old counter incremented on every
> such conflict, whereas the CB_GETATTR counter only counts callbacks that
> are actually queued, so concurrent conflicts that coalesce onto an
> already in-flight CB_GETATTR are now counted once rather than once per
> conflict. Report the procfs "wdeleg_getattr" line from the CB_GETATTR
> counter and drop the now-redundant NFSD_STATS_WDELEG_GETATTR counter, its
> helper, and its increment site.
>
> Assisted-by: Claude:claude-opus-4-8
> Signed-off-by: Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org>
> ---
>  fs/nfsd/netns.h        | 12 +++++++++++-

>  fs/nfsd/stats.c        |  2 +-
>  fs/nfsd/stats.h        |  5 +++--
>  6 files changed, 35 insertions(+), 7 deletions(-)

I agree with the Sashiko review that this patch introduces a UAF

The loop emits an entry for every opcode in OP_CB_GETATTR..OP_CB_OFFLOAD,
but only opcodes with a nfsd4_callback_ops instance get incremented (via
nfsd4_run_cb() keyed on cb->cb_ops->opcode). Opcodes nfsd never sends
correctly report 0. The exception is op 11 / CB_SEQUENCE: it's written by
encode_cb_sequence4args() on every minorversion>0 callback, so it goes
out on the wire but is never counted. The dump always shows op 11 with
count 0, which could be misread as "none sent" rather than "not tracked."

Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series converts the nfsstat server-side interfaces to use netlink instead of /proc/net/rpc/nfsd, which has several limitations. The new implementation adds per-network-namespace procedure call counts and exposes them through a new netlink handler in the nfsd generic netlink family. It also tracks callback operation counts and provides a procfs fallback for older kernels. The patch series includes six patches that make changes to various files in the kernel, including sunrpc, nfsd, and lockd.

2 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Chuck Lever Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Chuck Lever noted that the netlink handler in nfsd_netlink.c does not properly handle errors when sending a server-stats object, and requested that error handling be added to ensure that an error is returned when nla_put_*() calls fail. Reviewer Chuck Lever noted that the netlink handler does not stream across subsequent messages, instead emitting all server-stats objects in a single message on the first invocation, which contradicts the commit message's description of streaming design. The reviewer, Chuck Lever, pointed out that the server-stats object assembly in one message could lead to a buffer overflow issue when the assembled message exceeds the dump buffer size of NLMSG_GOODSIZE (approximately 3.7k on a 4k-page host). He noted that with LAST_NFS4_OP at 75, the proc4ops-ops loop alone emits around 2.1k bytes, and adding more NFSv4 operations would further increase the total size, potentially exceeding the buffer limit. The reviewer pointed out that the patch duplicates code from nfsd_nl_fill_proc_ops() and suggested generalizing a helper function to take the per-op counter source, reducing code duplication. Additionally, they noted that the loop emits entries for zero-count ops, which may not be intentional, and requested consideration of including the wdeleg_getattr counter in the netlink dump. Reviewer noted that when an nla_put fails, genlmsg_cancel() trims the partial message and skb->len becomes 0, causing -EMSGSIZE to be delivered as a terminal error instead of indicating a buffer full condition. He suggested building the dump in the same streaming pattern used by nfsd_nl_rpc_status_get_dumpit(), where one entry is emitted per message with the cursor saved in cb->args.
Show original comment 
A couple of questions on the dump design and one on the commit message,
then some smaller items.

The commit message says:

---

The code does not stream across subsequent messages.  The proc4ops-ops
nests are emitted in the first message, inside the same "if (start == 0)"
block as the scalars, and cb->args[0] only ever holds 0 or -1, never an
operation index.  The function's own kerneldoc agrees: "The entire
server-stats object is emitted in a single netlink message on the first
invocation."

Should the commit message be reworded to match the code, or should the code
move to the streaming design the message describes?  That second option ties
into the next question.

---

This handler assembles the entire server-stats object -- all scalars, the
proc2/3/4-ops nests, and one proc4ops-ops nest per NFSv4 operation -- in one
message under "if (start == 0)", then sets cb->args[0] = -1 so the next call
ends the dump.

Does that hold up once the assembled message no longer fits the dump buffer?
A dump skb is allocated at max(cb->min_dump_alloc, NLMSG_GOODSIZE), and this
handler sets neither cb->min_dump_alloc nor a .start callback, so the floor
is NLMSG_GOODSIZE, which is SKB_WITH_OVERHEAD(PAGE_SIZE), roughly 3.7k on a
4k-page host.  A larger skb is allocated only when userspace has previously
grown its receive buffer.

With LAST_NFS4_OP at 75, the proc4ops-ops loop alone emits 76 nests of about
28 bytes each (~2.1k); the proc2 and proc3 nests plus the scalar block bring
the total to roughly 3.5k -- already within a few hundred bytes of the floor,
and growing by one nest for every NFSv4 operation added later.

[ ... scalar puts for reply cache, fh, io, net, rpc snipped ... ]

---

This loop open-codes the same nest that nfsd_nl_fill_proc_ops() builds just
above -- nla_nest_start(), nla_put_u32(PROC_ENTRY_OP),
nla_put_u64_64bit(PROC_ENTRY_COUNT), nla_nest_end() -- into the same
NFSD_A_SERVER_STATS_PROC4OPS_OPS attribute.  Could the helper be generalized
to take the per-op counter source so this is not a second copy of the same
code?

The per-version block above skips empty versions:

	if (!prog->pg_vers[i] || !statp->vs_count[i])
		continue;

but this loop emits an entry for every op 0..LAST_NFS4_OP, zero-count ops
included.  Is that difference intentional?  Skipping zero counts here would
also trim the worst-case message size above.

There is also a counter that this dump does not emit.  /proc/net/rpc/nfsd
prints a wdeleg_getattr line after proc4ops:

	seq_printf(seq, "\nwdeleg_getattr %lld",
		percpu_counter_sum_positive(&nn->counter[NFSD_STATS_WDELEG_GETATTR]));

incremented by nfsd_stats_wdeleg_getattr_inc().  Since the goal is to expose
the statistics currently available via /proc/net/rpc/nfsd, should
wdeleg_getattr get an attribute here too, so nfsstat over netlink does not
drop it relative to the procfs path?

---

Back to the buffer question: when an nla_put eventually fails, control
reaches err_cancel, and genlmsg_cancel() trims the partial message back out,
so skb->len is 0 on return.  In netlink_dump():

	if (nlk->dump_done_errno == -EMSGSIZE && skb->len)
		nlk->dump_done_errno = skb->len;

the skb->len test fails, so -EMSGSIZE is delivered to userspace as a
terminal error rather than being taken as "buffer full, call again".  With
everything emitted under "if (start == 0)" and no per-attribute cursor in
cb->args, a retry rebuilds the same oversized message, so the dump cannot
ever complete.

nfsd_nl_rpc_status_get_dumpit() already follows the streaming pattern the
commit message describes: one entry per message, saving the cursor in
cb->args and returning skb->len on EMSGSIZE.  Would building this dump the
same way be more robust than the single-message form?

-- 
Chuck Lever

Signals: requested changes, error handling, contradiction between code and commit message
Chuck Lever Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
The reviewer noted that the code captures the cb_ops pointer and reads ops->opcode after nfsd4_queue_cb() returns, which is safe because every cb_ops points at a file-scope static const nfsd4_callback_ops structure that outlives cb. The reviewer suggested that the comment would read more accurately as snapshotting the cb_ops pointer rather than the opcode. Reviewer Chuck Lever noted that the increment of OP_CB_SEQUENCE count is triggered by minorversion > 0, including cases where no CB_SEQUENCE is sent on the wire, such as callback-channel probes and client teardown. He suggested moving the increment inside the if (ops) block to avoid this issue.
Show original comment 
The comment says the opcode is snapshotted before queuing, but the code
captures the cb_ops pointer and reads ops->opcode after nfsd4_queue_cb()
returns:

	if (ops)
		nfsd_stats_cb_op_inc(nn, ops->opcode);

That read is safe because every cb_ops points at a file-scope
static const nfsd4_callback_ops (nfsd4_cb_recall_ops,
nfsd4_cb_getattr_ops, and so on), which outlives cb, unlike cb and clp
themselves.  Would the comment read more accurately as snapshotting the
cb_ops pointer rather than the opcode, perhaps noting that ops stays
valid afterward because it points at static data?

---

Can this inflate the OP_CB_SEQUENCE count for callbacks that never send
a CB_SEQUENCE?  The increment fires whenever minorversion > 0, including
the ops == NULL case.  The only callback with cb_ops == NULL is the
null probe:

  nfsd4_init_cb(&clp->cl_cb_null, clp, NULL, NFSPROC4_CLNT_CB_NULL);

which nfsd4_probe_callback() and nfsd4_shutdown_callback() queue via
nfsd4_run_cb(&clp->cl_cb_null).  For a 4.1+ client, nfsd4_run_cb_work()
drops that work item without sending any RPC:

	/*
	 * Don't send probe messages for 4.1 or later.
	 */
	if (!cb->cb_ops && clp->cl_minorversion) {
		nfsd4_mark_cb_state(clp, NFSD4_CB_UP);
		nfsd41_destroy_cb(cb);
		return;
	}

So callback-channel probes, backchannel changes, and client teardown
each bump OP_CB_SEQUENCE for a minorversion > 0 client even though no
CB_SEQUENCE (and no callback at all) goes on the wire.

Would moving the CB_SEQUENCE increment inside the if (ops) block avoid
this?  A real callback on a 4.1+ client always prepends CB_SEQUENCE, and
the null probe carries cb_ops == NULL, so:

	if (queued) {
		if (ops) {
			nfsd_stats_cb_op_inc(nn, ops->opcode);
			if (minorversion > 0)
				nfsd_stats_cb_op_inc(nn, OP_CB_SEQUENCE);
		}
	} else {
		nfsd41_cb_inflight_end(clp);
	}

  • [20/20] nfsd: add support to CB_NOTIFY for dir attribute changes
Neutral LLM

This patch series adds support for CB_NOTIFY callbacks in directory delegations to the NFS server, enabling clients to register interest in a directory and receive notifications about changes within it without losing their lease.

3 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Jeff Layton Neutral LLM
This patch series addresses several issues and refactors code to prepare for CB_NOTIFY support. It adds data structures, allocation helpers, and callback operations needed for directory delegation notifications.

nfsd_lease_mng_ops. Do a quick check for that first when testing whether
the lease breaker owns the lease.

Jeff Layton Neutral LLM
This patch introduces RCU protection for fi_deleg_file to prevent concurrent access issues. It also refactors alloc_init_deleg into a common base with a pluggable sc_free callback.
Show original comment 
Add the necessary bits to nfs4_1.x and remove the duplicate definitions
from nfs4.h and the uapi nfs4 header. Regenerate the xdr files.

Note that regenerating these files caused conflicts with the definitions
of NFS4_VERIFIER_SIZE and NFS4_FHSIZE in include/uapi/linux/nfs4.h.
These constants are defined by the RFC, and are not part of the kernel
API. They have been removed. Userspace consumers who require those
constants should plan to get them from more authoritative sources.

The nfsstat4 enum defined in the .x is fed to the xdrgen-generated wire
encoder and decoder, which treat every enumerated value as legal on the
wire. Do not carry the NFS4ERR_FIRST_FREE sentinel (which is not a
protocol error code) into the .x; keeping it would make 10097 a value
that could leak onto the wire. Instead base nfsd's internal error codes
(NFSERR_EOF and friends) at an impossible nfsstat4 value, as lockd does
for its nlm__int__* status codes.

Signed-off-by: Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org>

2.54.0
Signals: WAITING_FOR_REVIEW
Jeff Layton Neutral LLM
This patch adds data structures and allocation helpers for handling CB_NOTIFY events. It also refactors alloc_init_deleg into a common base with a pluggable sc_free callback.
Show original comment 
RFC8881bis adds some new flags to GET_DIR_DELEGATION that later patches
will consume. In particular, Linux nfsd can't easily provide info about
directory cookies and ordering. The new flags allow it to omit that
information.

There is some risk here -- RFC8881bis is still a working group document,
and has been for years. The changes to directory delegations have been
stable for the last year or so however, so the hope is that those parts
won't change (much).

Signed-off-by: Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org>

This patch series addresses issues pointed out by Sashiko in review, specifically fixing problems related to CB_NOTIFY support in the NFS daemon. The patches fix a workqueue loop issue when the queue overflows, prevent a NULL dereference and use-after-free error in setup_notify_fhandle, and recall delegations if requested directory attribute changes cannot be encoded.

Last comment 2026-06-19 [PATCH v2] nfsd: recall deleg if a requested dir attr change can't be encoded Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch fixes a bug in the NFSv4 server where it silently drops directory attribute changes when they cannot be encoded, leading to stale cached directory attributes on the client side. The patch recalls the delegation when such an error occurs, as required by RFC 8881. It also distinguishes between genuine encode failures and benign cases where no specific dir attributes are requested.

[PATCH v3] nfsd: recall deleg if a requested dir attr change can't be encoded Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch fixes a bug in the NFS server where it silently drops directory attribute changes requested by clients if they cannot be encoded. The patch recalls delegations when such failures occur, as required by RFC 8881. It distinguishes between genuine encoding failures and benign cases where no attributes are requested. The patch also removes an unnecessary check and fixes NULL pointer handling issues.

Last comment 2026-06-15 [PATCH v5] nfsd: validate sockaddr length per family in listener_set Neutral LLM

This patch introduces a new helper function, nfsd_nl_validate_listeners(), to validate the listener list from userland before making any changes. The function checks each entry for well-formedness, including parsing against policy, carrying both an address and transport name, and ensuring the address length is sufficient for its family. This validation is done upfront to prevent malformed requests from causing side effects.

[PATCH nfs-utils] nfsstat: display NFSv4 callback operation statistics Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch adds a new feature to the nfsstat utility, which displays NFSv4 callback operation statistics. The kernel maintains per-operation call counts for NFSv4 backchannel operations and exports them through the server-stats netlink dump. The patch updates the nfsstat code to report these counts in a 'Server nfs v4 callback operations' section when the stats are fetched via netlink. This feature is only available over netlink, as the /proc interface does not expose equivalent data.

Last comment 2026-06-18 Re: kdevops-ng: graduating kdevops beyond Ansible Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch graduates kdevops-ng beyond its current reliance on Ansible, allowing for more flexible and efficient kernel testing. Reviewer noted that Windmill's architecture is complex and not well-positioned in the market, but believes it has useful features for developer workflow orchestration and suggested exploring its potential to turn kdevops into a kernel regression workflow engine. Reviewer Daniel Gomez expressed concerns about introducing too many new technologies at once (Nix and Windmill) and suggested a way forward: using Windmill as the replacement for Ansible, Makefiles, and Kconfig, while keeping the current toolkit for libvirt/libguestfs/Terraform paths. Reviewer noted that userland test suites are also important for kernel testing and suggested using Nix to build and pin custom versions of tools, allowing for consistent builds between host and guest.
Show original comment 
100%.

AFAIK, even Windmill itself struggles a bit to position what it is in
the market. But I think they get some obviously useful things right for
developer workflow orchestration that nobody else does. BTW, before
landing on this project I also looked at others: cijoe [1], Kestra,
and, at a different level, task.dev and just.systems. And these are the
alternatives Windmill compares itself against [2], in case any of them
are familiar to you (none were to me).

For a quick sense of what Windmill is, here's their architecture
diagram: https://www.windmill.dev/docs/misc/architecture

The way I think of it: a GitLab runner plus the YAML pipeline in
.gitlab-ci.yml (or the GitHub Actions equivalent), but for your
development workflows, not your project's CI. Though it can obviously be
used for project's CI too. All in one package, with far more pipeline
("flow", in Windmill terms) features [3]. That last part is one that I'm
also interested in: we could turn kdevops into a kernel regression
workflow engine with git-bisect and generate reports we can submit
directly to the mailing list and maintainers (similar to 0-day). Yes, we
can already do this today with Luis's patches from last year (I tried
them and they work), but it's exactly the kind of workflow where you can
see Ansible isn't the right tool.

Link: https://cijoe.readthedocs.io/en/latest/# [1]
Link: https://www.windmill.dev/docs/compared_to/peers [2]
Link: https://www.windmill.dev/docs/core_concepts#flow-specific-features [3]

---

I understand, and I'm 100% with you: Nix (+ Windmill) is simply too
many new things at once.

The real benefit here is that the guest and the controller run the exact
same environment. You could compare it to virtme-ng with overlayfs,
where, AFAIK, you boot a guest off the host's rootfs (read-only, with
an overlay for writes), so the guest mirrors the host. The difference is
that with virtme-ng that shared environment is still whatever Debian or
Fedora you happen to be running, so it isn't pinned or reproducible;
with Nix it's the same shared environment (same /nix/store).

A way forward could be to use Windmill as the Ansible, Makefile, Kconfig
replacement or the Nix-based paths, and keep the current toolkit for the
libvirt/libguestfs/Terraform paths. Both can coexist.

---

But we also need changes on userland test suites so I think it matters
too. I use Nix for exactly that, to build and pin custom versions
of the test suites and tools (e.g. fstests, fio, bcc, etc.) that I can
run either baremetal or in a VM. The benefit is that the host and guest
share the same /nix/store (the guest mounts it over virtiofs), so a tool
you build on the host is the exact same build the guest runs.

  • [10/10] docs: fuse: add io-uring buffer pool and zero-copy documentation Submitted 2026-06-12
Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series adds buffer pools and zero-copy to the fuse io-uring implementation, allowing servers to pass in a managed buffer pool that reduces memory usage requirements and enables future optimizations. The decoupling of ents and buffers also eliminates the need for dedicated payload buffers, reducing memory waste. Additionally, the series introduces zero-copy functionality, which allows direct access to client pages or page cache folios without copying data through an intermediary buffer, requiring CAP_SYS_ADMIN privileges and registered buffers.

2.52.0
View on lore ↗
Signals: acknowledged a fix is needed, agreed to restructure
Joanne Koong (author) Positive LLM
The author addressed a concern about the tight coupling between ents and buffers in fuse io-uring, explaining that they have decoupled them by allowing servers to pass in a buffer pool (a contiguous allocation of buffers) that the kernel will use for servicing ents/requests. The buffer ring logic is used to manage the buffer pool, selecting and recycling buffers on demand. The author provided detailed explanations of how the buffer pool is managed and how it reduces memory usage requirements.
Show original comment 
Right now, ents and buffers are tightly coupled in fuse io-uring where
each entry has its own dedicated payload buffer, requiring N buffers for
N entries where each buffer must be large enough to accomodate the
maximum payload size. This is suboptimal as most request types (lookup,
open, release, getattr, etc) require vastly less bytes than the maximum
payload size and some requests (unlink, rmdir, fsync, flush, etc) do not
require payload buffers at all.

Instead of requiring a 1:1 coupling between ents and payload buffers,
allow the server to pass in a buffer pool (a contiguous allocation of
buffers) that the kernel will use as it wishes for servicing
ents/requests. Buffers are now pooled and selected on demand. Entries
only "reserve" a buffer while actively processing a request that
requires a payload buffer. This decoupling reduces the memory usage
requirements needed to use fuse-io-uring. It allows for future
optimizations such as incremental buffer consumption which will allow
non-overlapping regions of a single buffer to be used simultaneously
across multiple ents/requests, and the possibility of multiple buffer
sizes to be supported per ring in the future.

To use this, the server must set the FUSE_URING_BUFPOOL flag during
registration and provide the payload buffer pool through the iovec
payload in the first sqe. The server must set init.buf_size and
init.nr_bufs in the struct fuse_uring_cmd_req it passes to the kernel to
describe the buffer pool configuration. Buffer pool usage is set on a
per-ring basis. All subsequent registration SQEs for the same ring must
pass in consistent flags.

Internally, a buffer ring data structure is used for managing the buffer
pool. The kernel manages buffer selection and recycling through this
ring structure. A payload buffer is selected from the ring on demand if
the request requires a payload buffer and recycled after the request
completes if used.

The contiguous buffer pool passed in is partitioned into individual
buffers according to the buf_size passed in at registration time.

  PAYLOAD BUFFER POOL (contiguous, provided via iov[1] in first sqe):
    |<-------------- payload_size ------------>|
    +--------- --+-----------+-----------+-----+
    |  buf [0]   |  buf [1]  |  buf [2]  | ... |
    |  buf_size  |  buf_size |  buf_size | ... |
    +--------- --+-----------+-----------+-----+

The buffer ring logic is as follows:
select:  buf = bufs[head % nbufs]; head++
recycle: bufs[tail % nbufs] = buf; tail++
empty:   tail == head (no buffers available)
full:    tail - head >= nbufs

Buffer ring request flow
------------------------
|  Kernel                                  |  FUSE daemon
|                                          |
|  [client request arrives]                |
|  >fuse_uring_send()                      |
|    [select payload buf from pool]        |
|    >fuse_uring_select_buffer()           |
|    [copy headers to ent]                 |
|    >copy_header_to_ring()                |
|    [copy payload to selected buf]        |
|    >fuse_uring_copy_to_ring()            |
|    [set buf_id in ent_in_out header]     |
|    >io_uring_cmd_done()                  |
|                                          |  [CQE received]
|                                          |  [read headers]
|                                          |  [read payload from buf_id]
|                                          |  [process request]
|                                          |  [write reply to header]
|                                          |  [write reply payload to
|                                          |    buf]
|                                          |  >io_uring_submit()
|                                          |   COMMIT_AND_FETCH
|  >fuse_uring_commit_fetch()              |
|    >fuse_uring_commit()                  |
|     [copy reply from ring]               |
|     >fuse_uring_recycle_buffer()         |
|    >fuse_uring_get_next_fuse_req()       |

Reviewed-by: Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org>
Reviewed-by: Baokun Li <libaokun@linux.alibaba.com>
Signed-off-by: Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com>

+/*
+ * sqe->addr is a ptr to an iovec array, iov[FUSE_URING_IOV_HEADERS] has the
+ * headers, iov[FUSE_URING_IOV_PAYLOAD] the payload
+ */
+static int fuse_uring_get_iovec_from_sqe(const struct io_uring_sqe *sqe,
+					 struct iovec iov[FUSE_URING_IOV_SEGS])
 {
+	struct iovec __user *uiov = u64_to_user_ptr(READ_ONCE(sqe->addr));
+	struct iov_iter iter;
+	ssize_t ret;
+
+	if (sqe->len != FUSE_URING_IOV_SEGS)
+		return -EINVAL;
+
+	/*
+	 * Direction for buffer access will actually be READ and WRITE,
+	 * using write for the import should include READ access as well.
+	 */

+			   FUSE_URING_IOV_SEGS, &iov, &iter);
+	if (ret < 0)
+		return ret;
+
+	return 0;
+}
+
+static int fuse_uring_bufpool_setup(struct io_uring_cmd *cmd,
+				    struct fuse_ring_queue *queue)
+{
+	const struct fuse_uring_cmd_req *cmd_req =
+		io_uring_sqe128_cmd(cmd->sqe, struct fuse_uring_cmd_req);
+	unsigned int buf_size = READ_ONCE(cmd_req->init.buf_size);
+	unsigned int nr_bufs = READ_ONCE(cmd_req->init.nr_bufs);
+	struct iovec iov[FUSE_URING_IOV_SEGS];
+	uintptr_t payload_addr;
+	size_t payload_size, ring_size;
+	struct fuse_bufpool *pool;
+	unsigned int i;
+	int err;
+
+	if (!buf_size || !nr_bufs)
+		return -EINVAL;
+
+	err = fuse_uring_get_iovec_from_sqe(cmd->sqe, iov);
+	if (err)
+		return err;
+
+	payload_addr = (uintptr_t)iov[FUSE_URING_IOV_PAYLOAD].iov_base;
+	payload_size = iov[FUSE_URING_IOV_PAYLOAD].iov_len;
+
+	if (payload_size < (uint64_t)buf_size * nr_bufs)
+		return -EINVAL;
+
+	/* create the pool, which is managed as a ring buffer */
+	ring_size = struct_size(pool, bufs, nr_bufs);
+	pool = kzalloc(ring_size, GFP_KERNEL_ACCOUNT);
+	if (!pool)
+		return -ENOMEM;
+
+	/* populate the ring buffer */
+	for (i = 0; i < nr_bufs; i++, payload_addr += buf_size) {
+		struct fuse_bufpool_buf *buf = &pool->bufs[i];
+
+		buf->addr = payload_addr;
+		buf->len = buf_size;
+		buf->id = i;
+	}
+
+	pool->nbufs = nr_bufs;
+	pool->tail = nr_bufs;
+
+	queue->bufpool = pool;
+
+	return 0;
+}
+
+/*
+ * If the queue is already registered, check that the queue was initialized with
+ * the same init flags set for this FUSE_IO_URING_CMD_REGISTER cmd. all
+ * FUSE_IO_URING_CMD_REGISTER cmds should have the same init fields set on a
+ * per-queue basis.
+ */
+static bool queue_init_flags_consistent(struct fuse_ring_queue *queue,
+					u64 init_flags)
+{
+	bool bufpool = init_flags & FUSE_URING_BUFPOOL;
+
+	return bufpool_enabled(queue) == bufpool;
+}
+

+static bool fuse_uring_req_has_payload(struct fuse_req *req)
+{
+	struct fuse_args *args = req->args;
+
+	return args->in_numargs > 1 || args->out_numargs;
+}
+
+static int fuse_uring_select_buffer(struct fuse_ring_ent *ent)
+{
+	struct fuse_ring_queue *queue = ent->queue;
+	struct fuse_bufpool *pool = queue->bufpool;
+	struct fuse_bufpool_buf *buf;
+	unsigned int tail = pool->tail, head = pool->head;
+
+	lockdep_assert_held(&queue->lock);
+
+	if (tail == head)
+		return -ENOBUFS;
+
+	buf = &pool->bufs[head % pool->nbufs];
+	pool->head++;
+
+	ent->payload_buf = *buf;
+
+	return 0;
+}
+
+static void fuse_uring_recycle_buffer(struct fuse_ring_ent *ent)
+{
+	struct fuse_bufpool_buf *ent_payload = &ent->payload_buf;
+	struct fuse_ring_queue *queue = ent->queue;
+	struct fuse_bufpool_buf *buf;
+	struct fuse_bufpool *pool;
+
+	lockdep_assert_held(&queue->lock);
+
+	if (!bufpool_enabled(queue) || !ent_payload->addr)
+		return;
+
+	pool = queue->bufpool;
+
+	/* pool should never be full */
+	WARN_ON_ONCE(pool->tail - pool->head >= pool->nbufs);
+
+	buf = &pool->bufs[(pool->tail) % pool->nbufs];
+	*buf = *ent_payload;
+
+	pool->tail++;
+
+	memset(ent_payload, 0, sizeof(*ent_payload));
+}
+
+static int fuse_uring_next_req_update_buffer(struct fuse_ring_ent *ent,
+					     struct fuse_req *req)
+{
+	bool buffer_selected;
+	bool has_payload;
+
+	if (!bufpool_enabled(ent->queue))
+		return 0;
+
+	buffer_selected = !!ent->payload_buf.addr;
+	has_payload = fuse_uring_req_has_payload(req);
+
+	if (has_payload && !buffer_selected)
+		return fuse_uring_select_buffer(ent);
+
+	if (!has_payload && buffer_selected)
+		fuse_uring_recycle_buffer(ent);
+
+	return 0;
+}
+
+static int fuse_uring_prep_buffer(struct fuse_ring_ent *ent,
+				  struct fuse_req *req)
+{
+	if (!bufpool_enabled(ent->queue))
+		return 0;
+
+	/* no payload to copy, can skip selecting a buffer */
+	if (!fuse_uring_req_has_payload(req))
+		return 0;
+
+	return fuse_uring_select_buffer(ent);
+}

2.52.0
View on lore ↗
Signals: acknowledged a fix, explained reasoning
Joanne Koong (author) Neutral LLM
The author addressed a concern about the handling of registered buffers in the buffer pool setup code, explaining that the pages backing the buffers are pinned upfront when registering buffers with io-uring, and that this eliminates the overhead of pinning/unpinning user pages. The author also clarified the requirements for using registered buffers, including setting the FUSE_URING_REGISTERED_BUFFERS init flag during registration and following standard protocol for using registered buffers.
Show original comment 
Allow servers to use an io-uring registered buffer as the buffer pool.
When the server registers buffers with io-uring, the pages backing the
buffers are pinned upfront. This eliminates the overhead of
pinning/unpinning user pages and translating virtual addresses per i/o
request. This also allows servers to use the same registered buffers for
subsequent backing store I/O (eg read_fixed/write_fixed), keeping data
in the same pinned pages without additional pinning or mapping overhead
required.

Registered buffers require the buffer pool (FUSE_URING_BUFPOOL) to be
enabled. To use registered buffers, the server needs to register the
payload buffers through io-uring and during registration, set the
FUSE_URING_REGISTERED_BUFFERS init flag (alongside the
FUSE_URING_BUFPOOL flag) in the registration sqe. For all sqes the
server passes in (including the registration ones), it needs to follow
standard protocol for using registered buffers (eg set
sqe->uring_cmd_flags to include IORING_URING_CMD_FIXED, and pass in the
index where the registered buffers reside to sqe->buf_index). Kernel
support for registered buffers is advertised to userspace via the
FUSE_HAS_IO_URING_BUFPOOL init flag (a single umbrella flag).

In benchmarks using passthrough_hp on a high-performance NVMe-backed
system, using registered buffers showed around a 10% throughput
improvement for direct randreads (~2150 MiB/s to ~2400 MiB/s), a 4%
improvement for direct sequential reads (~2510 MiB/s to ~2620 MiB/s),
a 8% improvement for buffered randreads (~2100 MiB/s to ~2280 MiB/s),
and a 6% improvement for buffered sequential reads (~2500 MiB/s to
~2670 MiB/s).

Reviewed-by: Baokun Li <libaokun@linux.alibaba.com>
Signed-off-by: Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com>

2.52.0
View on lore ↗
Signals: clarification, explanation
Joanne Koong (author) Neutral LLM
The author is addressing a concern about the zero-copy implementation, specifically the need for CAP_SYS_ADMIN and FUSE_URING_REGISTERED_BUFFERS flags to enable zero-copy. The author explains that these flags are necessary because zero-copy allows the server direct access to the client's underlying pages, rather than operating on an intermediary buffer. They also provide detailed information about the request flow for both the write and read paths, including how page-backed and non-page-backed args are handled.
Show original comment 
Implement zero-copy for fuse io-uring, eliminating memory copies between
userspace, the kernel, and the fuse server for read/write operations.
The server can directly access client pages or page cache folios without
copying data through an intermediary buffer. When a fuse request arrives,
the kernel registers the relevant pages into a sparse slot in the
server's io_uring registered buffer table. The server can then operate
on these pages directly using io-uring fixed buffer operations (eg
read_fixed/write_fixed) and the kernel unregisters these pages when the
request completes. Non-page-backed args (eg op out headers) will go
through the payload buffer as normal.

This requires CAP_SYS_ADMIN and FUSE_URING_REGISTERED_BUFFERS (which
requires using bufpools). Gating on registered buffers keeps the
configuration space small and avoids partially-optimized modes that are
unlikely to be useful in practice. This is gated behind CAP_SYS_ADMIN
because zero-copy allows the server direct access to the client's
underlying pages, rather than operating on an intermediary buffer that
the contents of the client's pages were copied into or on page cache
folios. Kernel support for zero copy is advertised to userspace via the
FUSE_HAS_IO_URING_BUFPOOL init flag (a single umbrella flag).

The request flow for the zero-copy write path (client writes data,
server reads it) is as follows:
=======================================================================
|  Kernel                                   |  FUSE server
|                                           |
|  "write(fd, buf, 1MB)"                    |
|                                           |
|  >sys_write()                             |
|    >fuse_file_write_iter()                |
|      >fuse_send_one()                     |
|        [req->args->in_pages = true]       |
|        [folios hold client write data]    |
|                                           |
|  >fuse_uring_copy_to_ring()               |
|    >copy_header_to_ring(IN_OUT)           |
|      [memcpy fuse_in_header]              |
|    >copy_header_to_ring(OP)               |
|      [memcpy write_in header]             |
|                                           |
|    >fuse_uring_args_to_ring()             |
|      >setup_fuse_copy_state()             |
|        [skip_folio_copy = true]           |
|                                           |
|      >fuse_uring_set_up_zero_copy()       |
|        [folio_get for each client folio]  |
|        [build bio_vec array from folios]  |
|        >io_buffer_register_bvec()         |
|          [register pages at
                 ent->zero_copy_index]      |
|        [ent->zero_copied = true]          |
|                                           |
|      >fuse_copy_args()                    |
|        [skip_folio_copy => return 0       |
|         for page arg, skip data copy]     |
|                                           |
|    >copy_header_to_ring(RING_ENT)         |
|      [memcpy ent_in_out]                  |
|    >io_uring_cmd_done()                   |
|                                           |
|                                           | [CQE received]
|                                           |
|                                           | [issue io_uring READ at
|                                           |  ent->zero_copy_index]
|                                           | [reads directly from
|                                           |client's pages (ZERO_COPY)]
|                                           |
|                                           | [write data to backing
|                                           | store]
|                                           |  [submit COMMIT AND FETCH]
|                                           |
|  >fuse_uring_commit_fetch()               |
|    >fuse_uring_commit()                   |
|      >fuse_uring_copy_from_ring()         |
|    >fuse_uring_req_end()                  |
|      >io_buffer_unregister(ent->zero_copy_index) |
|        [unregister sparse buffer]         |
|      >fuse_zero_copy_release()            |
|        [folio_put for each folio]         |
|      [ent->zero_copied = false]           |
|      >fuse_request_end()                  |
|        [wake up client]                   |

The zero-copy read path is analogous.

Some requests may have both page-backed args and non-page-backed args.
For these requests, the page-backed args are zero-copied while the
non-page-backed args are copied to the buffer selected from the buffer
ring:
    zero-copy: pages registered via io_buffer_register_bvec()
    non-page-backed: copied to payload buffer via fuse_copy_args()

For a request whose payload is zero-copied, the
registration/unregistration path looks like:

    register:  fuse_uring_set_up_zero_copy()
                 folio_get() for each folio
                 io_buffer_register_bvec(ent->zero_copy_index)

    unregister: fuse_uring_req_end()
                  io_buffer_unregister(ent->zero_copy_index)
                  -> fuse_zero_copy_release() callback
                     folio_put() for each folio

Please note that on abort for in-flight zero-copied requests that have
been sent to userspace, the registered bvec slot remains occupied and
its folios remain pinned until the io-uring ring is destroyed, at which
point io-uring unregisters all buffers and the fuse_zero_copy_release()
callback drops the folio references. Unregistering at teardown would
require operating on the ring context directly, whose validity is hard
to ascertain; this is deemed not worth the complexity for the abort
race, since everything is freed when the ring is torn down.

The throughput improvement from zero-copy depends on how much of the
per-request latency is spent on data copying vs backing I/O. When
backing I/O dominates, the saved memcpy is a negligible fraction of
overall latency. Please also note that for the server to read/write
into the zero-copied pages, the read/write must go through io-uring
as an IORING_OP_READ_FIXED / IORING_OP_WRITE_FIXED operation. If the
server's backing I/O is instantaneous (eg served from cache), the
overhead of the additional io_uring operation may negate the savings
from eliminating the memcpy.

In benchmarks using passthrough_hp on a high-performance NVMe-backed
system, zero-copy showed around a 35% throughput improvement for direct
randreads (~2150 MiB/s to ~2900 MiB/s), a 15% improvement for direct
sequential reads (~2510 MiB/s to ~2900 MiB/s), a 15% improvement for
buffered randreads (~2100 MiB/s to ~2470 MiB/s), and a 10% improvement
for buffered sequential reads (~2500 MiB/s to ~2750 MiB/s).

The benchmarks were run using:
  fio --name=test_run --ioengine=sync --rw=rand{read,write} --bs=1M
  --size=1G --numjobs=2 --ramp_time=30 --group_reporting=1

Reviewed-by: Baokun Li <libaokun@linux.alibaba.com>
Signed-off-by: Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com>

+static void fuse_zero_copy_release(void *priv)
+{
+	struct fuse_zero_copy_bvs *zc_bvs = priv;
+	unsigned int i;
+
+	for (i = 0; i < zc_bvs->nr_bvs; i++)
+		folio_put(page_folio(zc_bvs->bvs[i].bv_page));
+
+	kfree(zc_bvs);
+}
+
+static int fuse_uring_set_up_zero_copy(struct fuse_ring_ent *ent,
+				       struct fuse_req *req,
+				       unsigned int issue_flags)
+{

+	int err, i, ddir = 0;
+	struct fuse_zero_copy_bvs *zc_bvs;
+	struct bio_vec *bvs;
+
+	/* out_pages indicates a read, in_pages indicates a write */
+	if (req->args->out_pages)
+		ddir |= IO_BUF_DEST;
+	if (req->args->in_pages)
+		ddir |= IO_BUF_SOURCE;
+
+	WARN_ON_ONCE(!ddir);
+
+	ap = container_of(req->args, typeof(*ap), args);
+
+	zc_bvs = kmalloc(struct_size(zc_bvs, bvs, ap->num_folios),
+			 GFP_KERNEL_ACCOUNT);
+	if (!zc_bvs)
+		return -ENOMEM;
+
+	zc_bvs->nr_bvs = ap->num_folios;
+	bvs = zc_bvs->bvs;
+	for (i = 0; i < ap->num_folios; i++) {
+		bvs[i].bv_page = folio_page(ap->folios[i], 0);
+		bvs[i].bv_offset = ap->descs[i].offset;
+		bvs[i].bv_len = ap->descs[i].length;
+		folio_get(ap->folios[i]);
+	}
+
+	err = io_buffer_register_bvec(ent->cmd, bvs, ap->num_folios,
+				      fuse_zero_copy_release, zc_bvs,
+				      ddir, ent->zero_copy_index,
+				      issue_flags);
+	if (err) {
+		fuse_zero_copy_release(zc_bvs);
+		return err;
+	}
+
+	ent->zero_copied = true;
+
+	return 0;
+}

View on lore ↗
Signals: lacking, suggested
Joanne Koong (author) ↳ replying to Miklos Szeredi Neutral LLM
Author confirmed that pinned pages are accounted and limited by the kernel's io-uring infrastructure, addressing concerns about memory usage. The author addressed Miklos Szeredi's concern that registered buffers and buffer pools are mutually exclusive, explaining that the buffer pool feature is simpler to implement and reduces testing surface area.
Show original comment 
Yes, pinned pages are accounted and limited by the kernel io-uring
infrastructure (in io_account_mem() in io_uring/rsrc.c) when userspace
registers the buffers in io-uring.

---

There's no technical reason it can't but it keeps things simpler (with
the buffer pool, userspace can more optimally register the bufferpool
into one fixed slot since the buffers are contiguous), reduces the
surface area of configurations that need to be tested, and allows the
feature to be advertised under the FUSE_URING_BUFPOOL capability flag
during init instead of needing a separate flag. I think in practice,
servers that would want the benefits from registered buffers would
also want the benefits from buffer pools, so I don't think we're
leaving out a wanted use case.

Thanks,
Joanne
View on lore ↗
Signals: acknowledged, confirmed, acknowledged a concern
Joanne Koong (author) ↳ replying to Miklos Szeredi Neutral LLM
The author is addressing a concern about queue setup in the io-uring buffer pool patch series, proposing to add a new command operation (FUSE_IO_URING_CMD_QUEUE_SETUP) to pass in queue-level settings, including flags and number of queues. This would create the queue upfront instead of lazily creating it when the first sqe gets registered, adding some friction on the userspace side but considered a reasonable tradeoff.
Show original comment 
Looking at Bernd's reduced queue series [1], I think we'd most likely
want to also pass in the number of reduced queues separate from sqe
registration. I think along the lines of the config sqe Bernd brought
up, it seems cleanest to add a new cmd op
(FUSE_IO_URING_CMD_QUEUE_SETUP) where queue-level settings (eg flags,
number of queues, bufpool, etc) gets passed in. This would have the
nice side effect of doing the queue creation in this op instead of
right now creating the queue lazily when the first sqe gets
registered. On the userspace side, this adds a bit more friction (eg a
two-phase setup where userspace submits the queue setup cmd, reaps its
completion, and reuses that sqe to register an entry), but that seems
like a reasonable tradeoff.

Does this sound ok to you and Bernd?

Thanks,
Joanne

[1] https://lore.kernel.org/fuse-devel/20260529-reduced-nr-ring-queues_3-v5-0-1dc08c2fccf6@bsbernd.com/T/#u
View on lore ↗
Signals: considered a reasonable tradeoff
Joanne Koong (author) ↳ replying to Miklos Szeredi Neutral LLM
Author addressed a concern about how the server would access buffers in the pool, explaining that it knows the starting address and size of each buffer, allowing it to compute the correct address from the buf_id. Author addressed Miklos Szeredi's concern about the space efficiency of returning buf_id instead of buffer address in struct fuse_uring_ent_in_out, proposing a compromise to return a uint32_t offset that corresponds to the offset in the bufpool. Author agrees to add uAPI documentation for the new functionality, confirming the issue is resolved.
Show original comment 
The server knows the starting address of the bufpool (since it
allocated it) and it knows the size of each buffer in the pool. It can
compute from the buf_id the address corresponding to the buffer.

---

This would have to be returned as part of the struct
fuse_uring_ent_in_out. There's currently 12 bytes that are free to
use. Returning buf_id takes up 2 bytes and I think is better than
using 8 bytes to provide the buffer address. However, returning the
buf id makes adding incremental buffer consumption in the future a bit
trickier (eg the server would need to keep track of the offset into
the previously used buffer and do any alignment rounding up). Maybe a
good compromise is returning a uint32_t offset that corresponds to the
offset in the bufpool? The buf size is a uint32_t, so I think this
would work just as well as providing the buffer address directly.

---

That sounds good. I'll write some uAPI documentation for this and add
it to that new directory.

Thanks,
Joanne
View on lore ↗
Signals: acknowledged feedback, provided explanation, clarifying question
Joanne Koong (author) ↳ replying to Miklos Szeredi Neutral LLM
The author addressed a concern about the FIXED_READ call in io-uring, explaining that it takes a buf_index instead of an address and clarifying how userspace passes the ent->zero_copy_index to the kernel.
Show original comment 
The userspace FIXED_READ call (eg set up usually through the liburing
api io_uring_prep_read_fixed() [1]) takes a buf_index, not an address.
Userspace would pass the ent->zero_copy_index as the buf index.
ent->zero_copy_index corresponds to the fixed buffer where userspace
told the kernel (at sqe registration time through the "uint16_t
zero_copy_index" init param) to place the zero-copied pages for that
sqe.

Thanks,
Joanne

[1] https://man7.org/linux/man-pages/man3/io_uring_prep_read_fixed.3.html

> zero_copy_index" init param) to place the zero-copied pages for that
> sqe.

Okay so there are two buffers per sqe, one which is corresponds to a
real userspace address, and a zero copy one that is (by definition)
kernel-only and doesn't actually have an address.

Passing a NULL address to the fixed op seems okay in this case, but
that means the offset within the buffer is always zero.

Or am I misunderstanding something?

Miklos

Re: [PATCH v4 07/10] fuse: add io-uring buffer pools (implemented as buffer ring) Neutral LLM
Last comment 2026-06-19 Re: [PATCH v4 07/10] fuse: add io-uring buffer pools (implemented as buffer ring) Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch adds support for io-uring buffer pools to the fuse file system, allowing userspace to pass a chunk of memory to the kernel and offloading management logic. The approach taken is to implement buffer rings, which can be used for both metadata operations and backend IO requests. However, the need for multiple pools per queue is questioned, with the suggestion that dynamically adding new queues could provide more concurrency and capacity as needed.

5 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Miklos Szeredi ↳ replying to Joanne Koong Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer questioned the need for partitioning memory into buffers upfront and suggested simplifying the interface by removing redundant buffer size information. Reviewer suggested separating buffer registration into a separate operation as an initial step towards supporting multiple bufpools, and noted that this does not require immediate support for multiple bufpools but is beneficial for future-proofing Reviewer noted that 'queue' is ambiguous in the context of fuse and io-uring, and suggested clarifying its meaning to avoid confusion.
Show original comment 
Hmm, so why partition this memory into buffers up front?

We could just as well say that managing that chunk of  memory is up to
the kernel.  This would simplify the interface: the buffer size is
redundant, since it's the same as the max I/O size, no?

---

Yeah, and separating buffer registration into a separate op would be
one step in that direction.   It doesn't have to support multiple
bufpools from the start, but splitting out separate functions during
initialization is a good idea regardless.

---

Note, "queue" could mean two things here:

 - fuse's waiting list
 - cqe's on the ring

Currently there's 1:1 relation between those, but that's not mandatory
going forward.  So we could have multiple waiting queues for different
types of requests, but a single uring for sending them to the server.

Thanks,
Miklos

She asks if she's missing something and also questions the feasibility of implementing threadpools on the userspace side.
Show original comment 
That's a good point, especially since we can just return back offset
into the bufpools instead of buffer id. That's a much cleaner
interface. Though I guess if we want to be forwards-compatible with
having the option of multiple bufpools per queue in the future, we'd

I'm not sure I agree that splitting it out would be a good idea if we

Even with separate waiting queues and the kernel prioritizing sending
metadata requests before io, isn't there the issue that if the
requests/entries on the ring are all IO requests and then a storm of
metadata ops come in, they're still backed up waiting for the IO
requests to be finished? Or am I missing something?

On the userspace side, I think they'd need a threadpool which would
add extra context-switching overhead and would have to reserve a few
threads in that pool just for non-IO requests, and then have a way to
inspect requests in the ring to prioritize the non-IO ones, which
seems nontrivial. But maybe there's some simpler way to do this that
I'm not seeing.

Joanne

kernel and offloading the management logic entirely to the kernel.
Maybe I'm missing something obvious, but why would multiple pools per
queue still be needed? If the server needs more capacity, why not just
have the server spin up a new queue? That would have the included
benefit of adding more concurrency, and dynamically adding more queues
is a feature that would be added to fuse uring regardless. For
multipools in one queue, the pools would be discontiguous anyways,

incurs that much memory or cpu overhead, and imo the scenarios where a
server would want to just add more capacity but not parallelism are
uncommon.

requests. That solves it on the kernel side but the userspace side
would still need a threadpool of some sort if its backend IO blocks
its queue thread.

Joanne

Okay.  Could we have a variant of the _fixed API that takes an offset

Signals: NEEDS_WORK
Joanne Koong Neutral LLM
Proposed adding a flag to the _FIXED op to indicate that the offset is being supplied in place of the address, allowing unconditional passing of offsets.
Show original comment 
On Wed, Jun 17, 2026 at 7:48 AM Miklos Szeredi <miklos@szeredi.hu> wrote:
>
> On Wed, 17 Jun 2026 at 13:23, Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com> wrote:
>
> > For zero-copied registered buffers, the "address" you pass in is the
> > raw offset into the buffer (eg if you want to read into the
> > zero-copied buffer starting at offset 4k in the buffer, you would call
> > io_uring_prep_read_fixed(sqe, fd, (void *)4096, len, file_off,
> > buf_index);).
>
> Okay.  Could we have a variant of the _fixed API that takes an offset
> instead of an address?

This is a liburing API [1]. We could add a variant of the _fixed API
for this (will need to come up with a good name for it...). Internally
it would still have to set sqe->addr = offset, as in [2], but I think
this is a good idea to make it less confusing.

>
> That would work much better for the plain registered buffers too, as
> the fuse server wouldn't have to look up the address belonging to a
> particular buf_index, just supply the offset that was returned in cqe
> (as you suggested) to the _fixed op.

For plain (non-zero-copied) registered buffers, the fuse server would
still need to pass in the starting address of the buffer pool, so I
think it would just use the regular io_uring_prep_read_fixed() API and
pass in starting_address + offset. It's only for buffers that are
zero-copied (eg pages supplied by the kernel) that the addr is the
offset; for non-zero-copied buffers, the addr must be an actual uaddr.

Thanks,
Joanne

[1] https://github.com/axboe/liburing/blob/650d8fb7acb5952a12530b617f09da75b95c979c/src/include/liburing.h#L673
[2] https://github.com/axboe/liburing/blob/650d8fb7acb5952a12530b617f09da75b95c979c/src/include/liburing.h#L604
Signals: NEEDS_WORK
Miklos Szeredi ↳ replying to Joanne Koong Needs Work LLM
Expressed skepticism about the need for a flag, suggesting that the kernel could simply use stored and supplied uaddr's to calculate the offset.
Show original comment 
On Wed, 17 Jun 2026 at 14:17, Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com> wrote:

> For plain (non-zero-copied) registered buffers, the fuse server would
> still need to pass in the starting address of the buffer pool, so I

Why?  The kernel couldn't care less about the userspace address at

to calculate the offset into the buffer.

Signals: potential issue with libfuse
Askar Safin ↳ replying to Joanne Koong Neutral LLM
Expressed disappointment about the patch being reverted, but suggested an alternative solution to return EINVAL for writable pipe + SPLICE_F_NONBLOCK.
Show original comment 
Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com>:
> > speaking of fuse_dev_splice\u2026\u2026_write actually, this series has broken
> > xdg-document-portal!
> >
> > https://github.com/flatpak/xdg-desktop-portal/issues/2026
> >
> > Specifically what happens is that the EINVAL is returned due to oh.len
> > != nbytes:
> >
> > fuse_dev_do_write: oh.len 16400 != nbytes 15526
> >
> > (where 16400 == 16384 (read len) + 16, 15526 == 15510 (file len) + 16)
> >
> > After reverting the series, there is no error because oh.len
> > becomes 15526 too.
> 
> I think this is because of how libfuse handles eof / short reads. When
> it detects a short read, it fixes up the header length after the
> header was already vmspliced to the pipe because it assumes vmsplice
> mapped the header's page into the pipe by reference. It assumes that
> modifying the header length in place gets then reflected in what the
> pipe later splices out.
> 
> The logic for this happens in fuse_send_data_iov() [1]:
> a) sets out->len = headerlen (16) + len (16384) = 16400 in the
> stack-allocated fuse_out_header
> b) vmsplices the header to the pipe
> c) splices the backing file to the pipe. if this hits EOF, it'll get
> back 15510 instead of 16384
> d) detects the short read [2], fixes up the stack out->len = 16 + 15510 = 15526
> e) splices the pipe to /dev/fuse
> 
> After this patch, step b) is a straight copy which means step d)'s
> fixup doesn't modify what's in the pipe. This could be fixed up in
> libfuse to not depend on modify-after-vmsplice, but I don't think this
> helps for applications using already-released libfuse versions. I
> think this patch needs to be reverted.
> 
> Thanks,
> Joanne
> 
> [1] https://github.com/libfuse/libfuse/blob/master/lib/fuse_lowlevel.c#L846
> [2] https://github.com/libfuse/libfuse/blob/master/lib/fuse_lowlevel.c#L956

Uh, this is very unfortunate. But I still want to remove vmsplice.
Maybe we can somehow save my patchsets? For example, let's return EINVAL
for this particular combination (writable pipe + SPLICE_F_NONBLOCK).

-- 
Askar Safin

Last comment 2026-06-17 [PATCH] mm/bpf_memcontrol: mark BPF memcg kfuncs static Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch marks several BPF memory control functions in bpf_memcontrol.c as static, since they are only referenced by BPF programs and resolved through BTF (Binary Interface Format). This change is made to remove unnecessary external linkage. The affected functions include bpf_get_root_mem_cgroup, bpf_get_mem_cgroup, bpf_put_mem_cgroup, bpf_mem_cgroup_vm_events, and bpf_mem_cgroup_usage. These changes are based on the introduction of BPF kfuncs in previous commits (5904db9891f8, 5c7db3239c9f, and 99430ab8b804). The patch is reported to fix a kernel test robot issue and closes a related bug report.

So it reads like bpf kfuncs can be static.

{

      "description": "Marking kfuncs as static could cause the compiler
      to optimize them away, breaking BPF verifier BTF resolution.",

      "reasoning": "The patch adds `static` to multiple `__bpf_kfunc`
      annotated functions that are not called anywhere in C
      code. Normally, a compiler would remove unused static functions or
      fail to expose them in object files, which could prevent the BTF
      ID generation logic (`resolve_btfids`) from finding them. However,
      the `__bpf_kfunc` macro expands to include `__used` and
      `__retain`, which strictly instructs the compiler and linker to
      preserve the functions in the object file regardless of
      usage. Additionally, `resolve_btfids` and `BTF_ID_FLAGS`
      successfully parse and resolve `STB_LOCAL` static symbols, making
      this change completely safe and correct.",

          "function_or_symbol": null,
          "line_range": null,
          "why_this_location_matters": "This applies to all the
          functions modified by the patch."
        }

This patch adds two new tracepoints to the Linux kernel, mm_lru_add_drain and mm_lru_add_drain_all, which allow tracing of LRU add drains and drain-all requests. The new tracepoints provide information about the CPU and lru_add batch count when an lru_add batch is drained, as well as whether callers of __lru_add_drain_all set the force flag for all CPUs.

[PATCH] mm/page_alloc: use existing highatomic reserves on the buddy fastpath Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch modifies the Linux kernel's page allocation system to allow high-priority non-blocking allocations to use existing MIGRATE_HIGHATOMIC reserves on the buddy fastpath without granting permission to grow these reserves. The patch introduces a new flag, ALLOC_HIGHATOMIC_RESERVE, which allows growing MIGRATE_HIGHATOMIC reserves, and updates the semantics of ALLOC_HIGHATOMIC to only allow accessing the reserves. This change improves performance for certain workloads by reducing highatomic misses.

[PATCH v2] mm/page_alloc: use existing highatomic reserves on the buddy fastpath Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch modifies the Linux kernel's page allocation mechanism to allow high-priority non-blocking allocations to use existing MIGRATE_HIGHATOMIC reserves on the buddy fastpath, rather than falling back to other migratetypes. The ALLOC_HIGHATOMIC flag is updated to only provide access to these reserves without permission to grow them, and a new flag ALLOC_HIGHATOMIC_RESERVE is introduced to specifically allow growing the reserves. This change improves performance in certain workloads by allowing eligible allocations to consume existing MIGRATE_HIGHATOMIC pageblocks.

Last comment 2026-06-15 [PATCH v3 0/3] arm64: cross-CPU NMI via SDEI Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series adds a new mechanism to deliver NMI (Non-Maskable Interrupt) across CPUs on arm64 architecture, using the SDEI (System Description Event Infrastructure) protocol. The goal is to enable interrupting a CPU that has its interrupts locally masked, which is necessary for certain debug and observability features such as system backtraces and crash dumps. The existing pseudo-NMI mechanism is too expensive in terms of performance overhead, so this new approach uses SDEI events delivered by firmware to reach the target CPU without modifying the interrupt mask/unmask hot path. This patch series does not add a hard-lockup detector but provides a way for the backtrace and crash-stop paths to actually reach a masked CPU.

4 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Puranjay Mohan Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
The reviewer noted that the initcall for ARM_SDEI_NMI runs unconditionally on arm64 systems without SDEI support, causing two warning messages to be printed during boot. They suggested either reducing the severity of the error message or skipping the probe when SDEI is not present.
Show original comment 
This initcall runs unconditionally whenever ARM_SDEI_NMI is built in,
which includes the many arm64 systems that have no SDEI at all. On
those, sdei_event_register() -> sdei_event_create() ->
invoke_sdei_fn() returns -EIO, and the core already complains:
    pr_warn("Failed to create event %u: %d\n", event_num, err);

(that one isn't gated on err != -EIO, unlike sdei_mask_local_cpu() & friends).
We then add a second pr_err() on top, so every boot on a non-SDEI box
with this config gets two alarming lines for what is just "no firmware
support". At minimum, don't shout for -EIO/-EOPNOTSUPP here. Better,
skip the probe when SDEI isn't present  there's no exported predicate
today, but -EIO is the de-facto one.

Puranjay Mohan Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer noted that panic_smp_self_stop() passes a NULL pointer to crash_save_cpu(), leading to a synchronous abort on arm64 due to dereferencing of regs, and suggested adding a check for NULL regs and either calling crash_setup_regs() or skipping the save if self-stop registers aren't worth having.
Show original comment 
panic_smp_self_stop() passes regs == NULL. If a second CPU panics
after the primary has already set crash_stop, it loses the panic_cpu
cmpxchg and calls panic_smp_self_stop(); if it was running with
interrupts masked it never took the stop IPI, so it gets here with
crash_stop == 1. crash is then true and we do crash_save_cpu(NULL,
cpu), which ends up in elf_core_copy_regs(), and on arm64 that is just

        *(struct user_pt_regs *)&(dest) = (regs)->user_regs;

so a straight NULL deref -> synchronous abort while we're in the
middle of crashing. The old local_cpu_stop() never called
crash_save_cpu(), so this is a new regression from the unification.

The comment above the function says NULL means "capture the current
context", but crash_save_cpu() doesn't do that, it just dereferences
regs. If that's the intent, materialise it:

        if (crash) {
                struct pt_regs local;

                if (!regs) {
                        crash_setup_regs(&local, NULL);
                        regs = &local;
                }
                crash_save_cpu(regs, cpu);
        }

  crash_setup_regs(..., NULL) is the existing "capture current" helper. Or just
  skip the save when regs is NULL if the self-stop registers aren't
worth having.

Signals: regression, NULL pointer
Kiryl Shutsemau (author) ↳ replying to Puranjay Mohan Needs Work LLM
The author acknowledged a NULL dereference bug and promised to fix it in version 4 of the patch. The author addressed a concern about the crash_save_cpu() function being called when the interrupted context is NULL, agreeing to drop the 'NULL means capture current' wording and instead only call it if regs is not NULL.
Show original comment 
Good catch, you're right \u2014 that's a real NULL deref, and a regression
from folding ipi_cpu_crash_stop() in (the old code only ran on the IPI
path, which always has regs).

Will be fixed in v4.

---

I went with skipping it. No architecture saves registers for the
self-stopping CPU -- the generic, arm and powerpc panic_smp_self_stop()
all just mark offline and spin, and arm64 did the same via
local_cpu_stop() before this series. crash_save_cpu() is only ever fed
the interrupted context, from the crash shootdown or the crashing CPU
itself. So:

	if (crash && regs)
		crash_save_cpu(regs, cpu);

and I dropped the "NULL means capture current" wording from the comment
rather than make it true.

While fixing this I noticed the same block broke the CONFIG_KEXEC_CORE=n
build: the unification replaced the original ipi_cpu_crash_stop()'s
#ifdef CONFIG_KEXEC_CORE with an IS_ENABLED() check, but <linux/kexec.h>
only declares crash_save_cpu() under CONFIG_KEXEC_CORE (and pulling in
<linux/crash_core.h> directly collides with kexec.h's own stubs), so the
dead-but-compiled call was an implicit-declaration error. Restored the
#ifdef around the crash_save_cpu() call.

Thanks for the review!

-- 
  Kiryl Shutsemau / Kirill A. Shutemov

[PATCH v3 1/3] firmware: arm_sdei: add SDEI_EVENT_SIGNAL support Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch adds a new function called sdei_event_signal() to the Linux kernel's firmware/arm_sdei module. This function signals a software-signalled event (event 0) to another processor element (PE), making it pending and delivering an NMI-like interrupt even if interrupts are masked on that PE. The function is designed to be safe for use in non-maskable interrupt (NMI) or crash contexts, as it does not take any locks. The patch also updates the include files to reflect the new function's existence.

Last comment 2026-06-15 [PATCH v3 2/3] drivers/firmware: add SDEI cross-CPU NMI service for arm64 Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch adds a cross-CPU NMI service for arm64 using the SDEI (System Description Event Infrastructure) software-signalled event mechanism. The goal is to deliver an NMI-like event to an interrupt-masked CPU without the overhead of the pseudo-NMI hot-path. This is achieved by registering a handler for event 0 and signaling it with sdei_event_signal(0, mpidr). The first user of this service is arch_trigger_cpumask_backtrace(), which can now reach masked CPUs. If SDEI is absent or not enabled, the patch falls back to the IPI path. This change also adds a new file drivers/firmware/arm_sdei_nmi.c and updates several other files.

Last comment 2026-06-15 [PATCH v3 3/3] arm64: escalate smp_send_stop() to an SDEI NMI as a last resort Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch adds a new mechanism to the Linux kernel on arm64 architecture to handle CPUs that are wedged and ignoring interrupts. When a CPU is wedged, it ignores stop IPIs and without pseudo-NMI there's no NMI IPI to escalate to. The patch introduces a third rung in smp_send_stop() that signals an SDEI event 0 on the remaining online CPUs, which firmware delivers regardless of the target's DAIF. This allows the CPU to be stopped even if it's ignoring interrupts. The patch also folds stop bookkeeping into a shared function arm64_nmi_cpu_stop().
