only declares crash_save_cpu() under CONFIG_KEXEC_CORE (and pulling in
<linux/crash_core.h> directly collides with kexec.h's own stubs), so the
dead-but-compiled call was an implicit-declaration error. Restored the
#ifdef around the crash_save_cpu() call.

Thanks for the review!

-- 
  Kiryl Shutsemau / Kirill A. Shutemov
View on lore ↗
Signals: acknowledged a bug, promised a fix
Last comment 2026-06-17 [PATCH v4 0/4] arm64: cross-CPU NMI via SDEI Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series adds a new mechanism to deliver NMI (Non-Maskable Interrupt) across CPUs on arm64 architecture using SDEI (System Description Event Infrastructure). This is necessary for certain debug and observability features, such as capturing CPU backtraces and crash dumps, which require interrupting a CPU that has its interrupts locally masked. The existing pseudo-NMI mechanism is too expensive in terms of performance overhead, so this new approach uses SDEI to deliver the NMI at a lower cost. This allows for more accurate debugging and observability features without introducing significant performance regressions.

Last comment 2026-06-17 [PATCH v4 1/4] firmware: arm_sdei: add sdei_is_present() Neutral LLM

This patch adds a new function `sdei_is_present()` to the Linux kernel, which checks whether SDEI firmware is present and usable on a system. The function is exposed as an optional consumer can skip registration of events from an unconditional initcall when there is no SDEI firmware.

Reviewed-by: Douglas Anderson <dianders@chromium.org>
[PATCH v4 2/4] firmware: arm_sdei: add SDEI_EVENT_SIGNAL support Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch adds a new function called sdei_event_signal() to the arm_sdei firmware driver, which signals a software-signalled event (event 0) to another processor element (PE). The function is designed to be safe to call from non-maskable interrupt (NMI) or crash context and takes no locks. It uses the SDEI_EVENT_SIGNAL call to deliver the event NMI-like even when interrupts are masked on the target PE. The patch also updates the include files to reflect the new function.

[PATCH v4 3/4] drivers/firmware: add SDEI cross-CPU NMI service for arm64 Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch adds a new feature to deliver NMI-like events to interrupt-masked CPUs on arm64 architecture using the SDEI (System Description Event Infrastructure) software-signalled event mechanism. The goal is to provide a more efficient way to trigger backtraces and detect hung tasks or soft-lockup dumps without incurring the pseudo-NMI hot-path cost. A new driver, arm_sdei_nmi.c, implements this feature by registering an SDEI handler for event 0 and using it to signal target CPUs with their MPIDR (Multiprocessor ID Register). The first user of this feature is arch_trigger_cpumask_backtrace(), which can now reach masked CPUs without falling back to the IPI path when SDEI is present. If SDEI is absent, the patch falls back to the original IPI path.

Last comment 2026-06-17 [PATCH v4 4/4] arm64: escalate smp_send_stop() to an SDEI NMI as a last resort Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch adds a new mechanism to the Linux kernel on arm64 architecture to handle CPU wedging by escalating smp_send_stop() to an SDEI NMI (Non-Maskable Interrupt) as a last resort. When a CPU is wedged with interrupts masked, it ignores stop IPIs and without pseudo-NMI there's no NMI IPI to escalate to. The patch introduces a third rung in smp_send_stop() that signals an SDEI event 0 at the remaining online CPUs, which firmware delivers regardless of the target's DAIF (Debug Access Interface Flag). This allows the CPU to reach a wedged CPU and acknowledge by going offline. The stop bookkeeping is also folded into a shared function arm64_nmi_cpu_stop() for consistency.

Ongoing Submitted 2026-06-12 Last comment 2026-06-16 ⏱ No recent activity [RFC PATCH v2 0/7] mm, swap: Virtual Swap Space (Swap Table Edition) 7 patches
Show 7 individual patches
  • [1/7] mm, swap: add virtual swap device infrastructure Submitted 2026-06-12
  • [2/7] mm, swap: support zswap and zeroswap as vswap backends Submitted 2026-06-12
  • [3/7] mm, swap: support physical swap as a vswap backend Submitted 2026-06-12
  • [4/7] mm, swap: only charge physical swap entries Submitted 2026-06-12
  • [5/7] mm, swap: add debugfs counters for vswap Submitted 2026-06-12
  • [6/7] mm, swap: defer memcg_table allocation on physical clusters Submitted 2026-06-12
  • [7/7] mm, swap: widen swap_info_struct max/pages to unsigned long Submitted 2026-06-12
Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series introduces Virtual Swap Space (Swap Table Edition), which aims to address the limitations of current swap design by decoupling swap entries from physical backing slots. This allows for more efficient use of disk space, especially in scenarios where zswap is used. The changes include rebasing to a newer mm-unstable tip, fixing assorted issues, and simplifying code. Additionally, the patch series introduces new features such as deferring memcg_table allocation on physical clusters until the first vswap-backing slot installs and widening swap_info_struct->max and ->pages to support up to 8 PB of swap space.

Hi Nhat,

Tier 1: VSWAP, Tier 2: ZSWAP ...

I don't see any problem applying the desired functionality with the
currently proposed mechanism and interface. With this, a user would be
assigned the default Virtual -> RAM swap tier, and the overall picture
becomes one where swap tiers are composed according to the priority
setting.

A few more thoughts came to mind.

Shakeel also proposed a per-tier max for the swap tier interface.

https://lore.kernel.org/linux-mm/aiw2p5ANjsQUCIHA@linux.dev/

However, for vswap, rather than treating it as a case for limiting the
amount via such a per-tier max, I think the current interface is the
better fit. (But, as Shakeel mentioned, if we only allow the limit
to be set to 0 or max, the usage could end up being the same. I'm still
thinking this part through.)

I have a few other thoughts as well, but I plan to raise those points in
the swap tier discussion thread instead. Please take a look at the
related thread, and let me know if you have any opinions. :)

And I'll share more if other thoughts come to mind

Thanks,
Youngjun Park

> >       appropriate points such as reclaim or refault.

Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series addresses performance issues in alloc_iova() caused by linear search, which can lead to soft lockups. It introduces a maple tree to index iova ranges, allowing alloc_iova() to have O(log n) complexity while maintaining similar memory usage. The patches also include self-tests for the iova code and were tested on various platforms.

Jason Gunthorpe ↳ replying to Rik Riel Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer noted that mas_erase() does not propagate ENOMEM errors to the caller, potentially leading to unexpected behavior in atomic contexts.
Show original comment 
Currently it never returns a failure to the caller. Look at mas_erase():

	entry = mas_state_walk(mas);
	if (!entry)
		return NULL;
[..]
	if (mas_is_err(mas))
		goto out;
[..]
out:
	mas_destroy(mas);
	return entry;

There is no propogation of ENOMEM, it returns success. No caller
checks for any error here either.

So I think the intention is that it cannot fail, yet it does have the
memory allocations and busted failure path. Hence asking Liam what it
should be, and what about an atomic context.

Perhaps this might be relying on the modern kernels "small allocations
never fail", meaning mas_erase never fails, but then you can't
call it from an atomic context..

In any case, it does look like you can't use mas_erase from an atomic
context anyhow so your prior option with the mas_store_gfp() and
failure handling seems reasonable.

Jason

Signals: requested changes
Liam Howlett ↳ replying to Jason Gunthorpe Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer noted that the patch does not include a necessary update to the iova_insert_rbtree() function to handle the new maple tree data structure. Reviewer noted that alloc_iova() previously being O(1) instead of O(log n) as claimed in the patch description, which could lead to incorrect assumptions about its performance Reviewer Liam Howlett noted that the current implementation of alloc_iova() does not propagate errors to the caller, specifically mentioning -ENOMEM as a possible failure case, and suggested returning -ENOMEM instead of NULL on failure. Reviewer noted that mas_store_gfp() can be optimized by writing XA_ZERO_ENTRY over existing entries and then performing a single mas_store_gfp() of NULL over the whole range, reducing tree rebalancing and improving performance.
Show original comment 
+Cc maple-tree list.

On 26/06/12 03:03PM, Jason Gunthorpe wrote:

---

At one point this was considered to be impossible to fail, and it is
documented to return the entry or null.

---

It _can_ fail, but right now that error will not be propagated to the
caller.  The caller could infer the failure due to the return of NULL...
if that's what would happen, so there's very much an issue on failure
that I need to investigate and fix.

It is possible that it fails with -ENOMEM.  And the spinlock can be
dropped if you are not using an external lock and the gfp flag allows
blocking.  On failure to allocate and the lock is dropped, we retry the
operation from the start in case there was a race with another writer.

I think I should probably change this to return -ENOMEM, fix the docs on
it, and probably audit the callers (most use external locks or are
early-boot-so-don't-worry-about-it).  Any issue here should also be
caught by lockdep pretty quickly.

---

mas_store_gfp() works if you know the range of your entry.  You could
also write the XA_ZERO_ENTRY over the entry so that there is no internal
node changes - just a value swap.  If you do this, you have to be
careful when reading things back when using the mas_ interface.

I think, in your case, hitting an XA_ZERO_ENTRY would be necessary to
indicate that we cannot reuse this particular location until it is
correctly dealt with?  Or is the maple tree the only reason it is
considered unusable?

One thing to remember is that each write can cause allocations to occur,
so if you have a list of items being overwritten then you are causing
the tree to do each write and potentially rebalancing (as you shrink the
data beyond the lower limit of the node).

One way around that is to write XA_ZERO_ENTRY over each one as you
deal with your entry.  Then, when you are done you do a single
mas_store_gfp() of NULL over the whole range.  It will be a larger tree
operation, but smaller than the incremental steps.

Thanks,
Liam

This is a case where there is no sane way to handle destroy

exposes a failable erase to it's caller..

IDK, could it handle it internally somehow? Or at least document a

Last comment 2026-06-16 [PATCH 0/3] mm: __access_remote_vm with per-VMA lock 3 patches
Show 3 individual patches
  • [1/3] x86/mm: use READ_ONCE/WRITE_ONCE for mm->context.untag_mask
  • [2/3] mm/pagewalk: let folio_walk_start() run under the per-VMA lock
  • [3/3] mm: read remote memory without the mmap lock where possible Neutral Last comment 2026-06-16 Suren Baghdasaryan
Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series introduces a fast path for __access_remote_vm that uses per-VMA locks instead of the process-wide mmap_lock, improving system monitoring reliability and speeding up __access_remote_vm under contention. This is made possible by the introduction of per-VMA locks in the kernel and is only enabled when it's safe to do so. The patch series includes code changes and benchmarking, but still requires further cleanup.

Suren Baghdasaryan Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
reviewer noted that using per-VMA locks in __access_remote_vm could lead to reading inconsistent data when the read spans multiple VMAs, as neighboring VMAs can be unmapped and remapped while the read is in progress Reviewer noted that Dave Hansen's patchset to remove CONFIG_PER_VMA_LOCK and make per-VMA locks always available would simplify the current patchset, effectively making it redundant.
Show original comment 
I don't think we should be using per-VMA locks if the read spans
multiple VMAs. Doing that would risk a possibility of reading
inconsistent data since we are locking one VMA at a time. While we
load and read VMA, its neighboring VMA can be unmapped and another one
can be mapped in its place. So, our read spanning both VMAs will
return inconsistent data. access_remote_vm_fast() can check if the
entire read is contained within one VMA and if not, fall back to
mmap_lock.

---

Just FYI, Dave Hansen is working on a patchset [1] that removes
CONFIG_PER_VMA_LOCK and makes per-VMA locks always available. This
would simplify this patchset too.

[1] https://lore.kernel.org/all/20260610230409.A44D29FA@davehans-spike.ostc.intel.com/
View on lore ↗
Signals: risk of reading inconsistent data, fall back to mmap_lock, patch redundancy
Last comment 2026-06-17 [PATCH] mm: khugepaged: free xarray nodes left behind by failed collapse_file() Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch addresses a memory leak issue in the khugepaged code, specifically when collapse_file() fails and leaves behind empty xarray nodes. These nodes are not freed on error paths, leading to their leakage until the inode is evicted. The patch introduces a mechanism to prune these empty nodes by storing an XA_RETRY_ENTRY into each slot and immediately clearing it, allowing the node and its ancestors to be freed.

Matthew Wilcox ↳ replying to Rik Riel Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Matthew Wilcox suggested deferring the cleanup of xarray nodes left behind by failed collapse_file() to clear_inode(), citing that such scenarios are rare and unlikely, and arguing that it's not worth the level of cleanup proposed.
Show original comment 
I think the ways to produce this problem are sufficiently rare/unlikely
to not merit this level of cleanup.  Why don't we defer it to
clear_inode() instead?

reasons not to:

1. the page cache is not the only user of xarrays and
theoretically either of these things could happen elsewhere

2. can a user do this on purpose to screw other users over?  i don't
think so, any more than they can dos the system by bringing a lot of
inodes into memory; we have cgroups and ulimits to protect against that

-	xas_unlock_irqrestore(&xas, flags);
+	xa_lock_irqsave(xa, flags);
+	__xa_destroy(xa);
+	xa_unlock_irqrestore(xa, flags);
 }
 EXPORT_SYMBOL(xa_destroy);

Last comment 2026-06-16 [PATCH v3] sched/mmcid: fix OOB clear_bit when CID is MM_CID_UNSET in fixup path Neutral LLM

This patch fixes a memory corruption bug in the Linux kernel's CID (Context ID) management system, specifically when clearing bits in the cid bitmap. The issue occurs when a task is running with an unset CID and a per-CPU -> per-task fixup walks over it, leading to an out-of-bounds write.

On 2026-06-16 16:38, Rik van Riel wrote:
> In mm_cid_fixup_cpus_to_tasks(), when rq->curr has the target mm and
> mm_cid.active is set, the CID is checked with cid_in_transit() before
> setting the transition bit.  In per-CPU mode a newly forked or exec'd
> task can be running with mm_cid.cid == MM_CID_UNSET because CIDs are
> assigned lazily on schedule-in.  With cid_in_transit() the guard passes

Show original comment 
Good idea, and it's certainly feasible. When I considered this a while
ago, the reasons I didn't take this direction were:

1. There's no real-world usage for adjusting the swap tier amount (it's
   either 0 or MAX). That said, your suggestion to initially allow only
   0 and max is the killing point, and it's making me reconsider.

2. The implementation cost seems high. The current implementation
   handles this at runtime via simple masking.

3. Relationship with swap.max:
   - If we tie it to the current interface, wouldn't limiting the swap
     amount within a selected tier already be possible? I wonder if
     that alone is enough.
   - If we add tier.max, it would need to be a subset of swap.max.
     (Any other complexities here?)

4. vswap enable/disable: vswap doesn't seem to have an amount-control
   aspect, so an on/off semantic would be clearer.
   https://lore.kernel.org/linux-mm/ai5kOOmR1LPTWs1J@yjaykim-PowerEdge-T330/T/#m8831ec057bf9387978d3bd698f51920600e09a04

In that case, the internal logic could stay roughly the same rather
than counting via a page counter. Something like:

1. Change the interface shell: tier.*.max \u2014 allow only 0 ~ max.
2. Keep the internal logic as is: 0 disables the mask (child memcgs
   off too), max enables it (child memcgs on too).
3. memory.zswap.max integrates naturally (it's memory."tier_name".max).
4. Extend later if use cases arise.

On balance I still lean toward the current interface, but if a per-tier
max is the better fit for memcg's direction and others feel the same,
I'm happy to switch. I'd like to hear Shakeel's thoughts again, and I'm
curious about others' opinions too.

A few more perspectives on the points below.

---

Let me clarify a part I wrote confusingly. Handling
memory.zswap.writeback via tiers is possible, but I don't think the
interface itself would be replaced even if memory.swap.tiers is adopted.

Selecting only zswap in memory.swap.tiers would not just disable
writeback.it would also block regular swap entirely, which differs
slightly from the current semantic. (... "Per the cgroup v2 docs: a
zswap-only tier setting is subtly different from setting
memory.swap.max to 0, since it still allows pages to be written to the
zswap pool; this has no effect if zswap is disabled, and swapping is
allowed unless memory.swap.max is set to 0.")

So the interface itself needs to be retained, and it could be extended
toward selective writeback \u2014 e.g., passing a desired tier into
memory.zswap.writeback so writeback targets only that tier. Currently
it only controls on/off. Other tiers probably don't need this. demotion
based on the selected tier should be enough.

Thanks,
Youngjun Park

Last comment 2026-06-16 [PATCH 0/2] block: invalidate cached plug timestamp on context switch 2 patches
Show 2 individual patches
  • [1/2] kernel/fork: clear PF_BLOCK_TS in copy_process()
  • [2/2] block: invalidate cached plug timestamp after task switch
Neutral LLM

This patch series addresses a problem where the cached plug timestamp is not invalidated on context switch, leading to incorrect throttling based on stale information. The patches introduce a new invariant that PF_BLOCK_TS implies current->plug != NULL and clear PF_BLOCK_TS in copy_process() to maintain this invariant. The second patch invalidates the cached plug timestamp after task switch.

[PATCH 0/1] sched/psi: skip irqtime accounting when no new irq time has elapsed
Show 1 individual patches
  • [1/1] sched/psi: skip irqtime accounting when no new irq time has elapsed
Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch optimizes the irqtime accounting in the Linux kernel's PSI (Protection Domain) scheduler by skipping unnecessary work when no new interrupt time has elapsed since the last update. The current implementation treats a zero delta as 'do the work', which is common and wasteful, especially on systems with many context switches across group boundaries. The patch extends an early return to cover this case, reducing unnecessary overhead and improving performance.

Last comment 2026-06-17 [PATCH] smp: Use release stores for csd_lock_record() state Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch updates the way diagnostic state is published in the Linux kernel's SMP (Symmetric Multi-Processing) code. It replaces open-coded memory barriers and plain stores with release operations, ensuring that the publication of per-CPU CSD (Component System Description) debug state is properly ordered. This change improves the accuracy of stall warnings by allowing the stall to end while diagnostics are being assembled, rather than requiring a precise completion/stall boundary. The patch also removes unnecessary full barriers in the clear path on x86 and uses release operations on weaker memory models.

3 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants

Reviews Given (5)
Re: [PATCH v2 07/11] hugetlb: replace filemap_lock_hugetlb_folio with filemap_lock_folio Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies
Re: [PATCH 3/3] mm: read remote memory without the mmap lock where possible Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch modifies the kernel's memory management to allow reading remote memory without acquiring the mmap lock in certain situations. The change addresses a performance issue related to /proc/PID/cmdline and /proc/PID/environ polling, where repeated reads through access_remote_vm() can trigger unwanted page reclaim behavior. To fix this, the patch adds a new fast path for resident pages that copies data without marking the folio as accessed, thus avoiding the slow path's equivalent folio_mark_accessed().

Re: [PATCH 3/3] mm: read remote memory without the mmap lock where possible Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies
Last comment 2026-06-18 Re: [PATCH] smp: Use release stores for csd_lock_record() state Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch modifies the csd_lock_record() function in the kernel's SMP (Symmetric Multi-Processing) code to use release stores for updating the cur_csd state. The change ensures that any preceding CSD callback or unlock is ordered before a remote reader observes the updated cur_csd pointer, preventing potential data corruption issues. The patch pairs with a corresponding load-acquire operation in csd_lock_wait_toolong() to guarantee visibility of the updated cur_csd state.
